<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Injury</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Physiological wound caused by an external source</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the legal sense of the term, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legal_injury" title="Legal injury">Legal injury</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Injury_(disambiguation)" title="Injury (disambiguation)">Injury (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Medical condition</div><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Medical_specialty" title="Medical specialty">Specialty</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Emergency_medicine" title="Emergency medicine">Emergency medicine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Traumatology" title="Traumatology">traumatology</a> <span class="penicon autoconfirmed-show"><a href="https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Q193078?uselang=en#P1995" title="Edit this on Wikidata"><img alt="Edit this on Wikidata" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="10" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8a/OOjs_UI_icon_edit-ltr-progressive.svg/15px-OOjs_UI_icon_edit-ltr-progressive.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8a/OOjs_UI_icon_edit-ltr-progressive.svg/20px-OOjs_UI_icon_edit-ltr-progressive.svg.png 2x" style="vertical-align: text-top" width="10"/></a></span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Complication_(medicine)" title="Complication (medicine)">Complications</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">Infection</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shock_(circulatory)" title="Shock (circulatory)">shock</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemorrhaging" title="Hemorrhaging">hemorrhaging</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organ_failure" title="Organ failure">organ failure</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Types</th><td class="infobox-data">Traumatic, burn, toxic, overuse</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>An <b>injury</b> is any <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physiological" title="Physiological">physiological</a> damage to living tissue<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> caused by immediate physical stress. An injury can occur <a href="/wiki/Intention" title="Intention">intentionally</a> or <a href="/wiki/Accident" title="Accident">unintentionally</a> and may be caused by <a href="/wiki/Blunt_trauma" title="Blunt trauma">blunt trauma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Penetrating_trauma" title="Penetrating trauma">penetrating trauma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burn" title="Burn">burning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toxin" title="Toxin">toxic exposure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asphyxia" title="Asphyxia">asphyxiation</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Overexertion" title="Overexertion">overexertion</a>. Injuries can occur in any part of the body, and different symptoms are associated with different injuries. 
</p><p>Treatment of a major injury is typically carried out by a <a href="/wiki/Health_professional" title="Health professional">health professional</a> and varies greatly depending on the nature of the injury. <a href="/wiki/Traffic_collision" title="Traffic collision">Traffic collisions</a> are the most common cause of accidental injury and injury-related death among humans. Injuries are distinct from <a href="/wiki/Chronic_condition" title="Chronic condition">chronic conditions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Psychological_trauma" title="Psychological trauma">psychological trauma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">infections</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Medical_procedure" title="Medical procedure">medical procedures</a>, though injury can be a contributing factor to any of these.
</p><p>Several major health organizations have established systems for the classification and description of human injuries. 
</p>

color:black;"> </span> 66-89</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffd820; color:black;"> </span> 90-114</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffc020; color:black;"> </span> 115-137</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffa020; color:black;"> </span> 138-171</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff9a20; color:black;"> </span> 172-193</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#f08015; color:black;"> </span> 194-226</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#e06815; color:black;"> </span> 227-291</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#d85010; color:black;"> </span> 292-379</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#d02010; color:white;"> </span> 380-2,730</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Injuries may be intentional or unintentional. Intentional injuries may be acts of violence against others or <a href="/wiki/Self-harm" title="Self-harm">self-harm</a> against one's own person. Accidental injuries may be unforeseeable or they may be caused by <a href="/wiki/Negligence" title="Negligence">negligence</a> The most common types of unintentional injuries in order are traffic accidents, falls, <a href="/wiki/Drowning" title="Drowning">drowning</a>, burns, and accidental poisoning. Certain types of injuries are more common in <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">developed countries</a> or <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing countries</a>. Traffic injuries are more likely to kill pedestrians than drivers in developing countries. Scalding burns are more common in developed countries, while open-flame injuries are more common in developing countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2021, approximately 4.4 million people are killed due to injuries each year worldwide, constituting nearly 8% of all deaths. 3.16 million of these injuries are unintentional and 1.25 million are intentional. Traffic accidents are the most common form of deadly injury, causing about one-third of injury-related deaths. One-sixth are caused by suicide, and one-tenth are caused by homicide. Tens of millions of individuals require medical treatment for nonfatal injuries each year, and injuries are responsible for about 10% of all years lived with disability. Men are twice as likely to be killed through injury than women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> In 2013, 367,000 children under the age of five died from injuries, down from 766,000 in 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GDB2013_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-GDB2013-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification_systems">Classification systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Injury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Classification systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) developed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Classification_of_External_Causes_of_Injury" title="International Classification of External Causes of Injury">International Classification of External Causes of Injury</a> (ICECI). Under this system, injuries are classified by mechanism of injury, objects/substances producing injury, place of occurrence, activity when injured, the role of human intent, and additional modules. These codes allow the identification of distributions of injuries in specific populations and case identification for more detailed research on causes and preventive efforts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>The United States <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Labor_Statistics" title="Bureau of Labor Statistics">Bureau of Labor Statistics</a> developed the Occupational Injury and Illness Classification System (OIICS). Under this system injuries are classified by nature, part of body affected, source and secondary source, and event or exposure. The OIICS was first published in 1992 and has been updated several times since.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Orchard_Sports_Injury_and_Illness_Classification_System_(OSIICS)" title="Orchard Sports Injury and Illness Classification System (OSIICS)">The Orchard Sports Injury and Illness Classification System (OSIICS)</a>, previously OSICS, is used to classify injuries to enable research into specific <a href="/wiki/Sports_injury" title="Sports injury">sports injuries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Injury_severity_score" title="Injury severity score">injury severity score</a> (ISS) is a medical score to assess trauma severity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Copes_et_al._10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Copes_et_al.-10">[10]</a></sup> It correlates with mortality, morbidity, and hospitalization time after trauma. It is used to define the term <i>major trauma</i> (<a href="/wiki/Polytrauma" title="Polytrauma">polytrauma</a>), recognized when the ISS is greater than 15.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Copes_et_al._10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Copes_et_al.-10">[10]</a></sup> The AIS Committee of the <a href="/wiki/Association_for_the_Advancement_of_Automotive_Medicine" title="Association for the Advancement of Automotive Medicine">Association for the Advancement of Automotive Medicine</a> designed and updates the scale.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanisms">Mechanisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Injury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Mechanisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trauma">Trauma</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Injury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Trauma">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Traumatic injury is caused by an external object making forceful contact with the body, resulting in a <a href="/wiki/Wound" title="Wound">wound</a>. <a href="/wiki/Major_trauma" title="Major trauma">Major trauma</a> is a severe traumatic injury that has the potential to cause <a href="/wiki/Disability" title="Disability">disability</a> or <a href="/wiki/Death" title="Death">death</a>. Serious traumatic injury most often occurs as a result of traffic collisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-11">[11]</a></sup> Traumatic injury is the leading cause of death in people under the age of 45.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Blunt trauma injuries are caused by the forceful impact of an external object. Injuries from blunt trauma may cause <a href="/wiki/Internal_bleeding" title="Internal bleeding">internal bleeding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bruise" title="Bruise">bruising</a> from ruptured <a href="/wiki/Capillary" title="Capillary">capillaries</a> beneath the skin, abrasion from scraping against the superficial <a href="/wiki/Epidermis" title="Epidermis">epidermis</a>, lacerated tears on the skin or internal organs, or <a href="/wiki/Bone_fracture" title="Bone fracture">bone fractures</a>. <a href="/wiki/Crush_injury" title="Crush injury">Crush injuries</a> are a severe form of blunt trauma damage that apply large force to a large area over a longer period of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-11">[11]</a></sup> Penetrating trauma injuries are caused by external objects entering the <a href="/wiki/Tissue_(biology)" title="Tissue (biology)">tissue</a> of the body through the <a href="/wiki/Human_skin" title="Human skin">skin</a>. Low-velocity penetration injuries are caused by sharp objects, such as <a href="/wiki/Stab_wound" title="Stab wound">stab wounds</a>, while high-velocity penetration injuries are caused by ballistic projectiles, such as <a href="/wiki/Gunshot_wound" title="Gunshot wound">gunshot wounds</a> or injuries caused by <a href="/wiki/Fragmentation_(weaponry)" title="Fragmentation (weaponry)">shell fragments</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Perforated injuries result in an entry wound and an exit wound, while puncture wounds result only in an entry wound. Puncture injuries result in a cavity in the tissue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Burns">Burns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Injury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Burns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Burns.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1029" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Burns.png/220px-Burns.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Burns.png/330px-Burns.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Burns.png/440px-Burns.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Burns.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Burns by severity</div></div></div>
<p>Burn injury is caused by contact with extreme temperature, chemicals, or radiation. The effects of burns vary depending on the depth and size. Superficial or first-degree burns only affect the <a href="/wiki/Epidermis" title="Epidermis">epidermis</a>, causing pain for a short period of time. Superficial partial-thickness burns cause weeping <a href="/wiki/Blister" title="Blister">blisters</a> and require dressing. Deep partial-thickness burns are dry and less painful due to the burning away of the skin and require surgery. Full-thickness or third-degree burns affect the entire <a href="/wiki/Dermis" title="Dermis">dermis</a> and is susceptible to <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">infection</a>. Fourth-degree burns reach deep tissues such as muscles and bones, causing loss of the affected area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Thermal_burn" title="Thermal burn">Thermal burns</a> are the most common type of burn, caused by contact with excessive heat, including contact with flame, contact with hot surfaces, or <a href="/wiki/Scalding" title="Scalding">scalding</a> burns caused by contact with hot water or steam. <a href="/wiki/Frostbite" title="Frostbite">Frostbite</a> is a type of burn caused by contact with excessive cold, causing cellular injury and deep tissue damage through the crystallization of water in the tissue. <a href="/wiki/Friction_burn" title="Friction burn">Friction burns</a> are caused by <a href="/wiki/Friction" title="Friction">friction</a> with external objects, resulting in a burn and abrasion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-15">[15]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Radiation_burn" title="Radiation burn">Radiation burns</a> are caused by exposure to <a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation" title="Ionizing radiation">ionizing radiation</a>. Most radiation burns are <a href="/wiki/Sunburn" title="Sunburn">sunburns</a> caused by <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> radiation or high exposure to radiation through medical treatments such as repeated <a href="/wiki/Radiography" title="Radiography">radiography</a> or <a href="/wiki/Radiation_therapy" title="Radiation therapy">radiation therapy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Electrical_burn" title="Electrical burn">Electrical burns</a> are caused by contact with <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a> as it enters and passes through the body. They are often deeper than other burns, affecting lower tissues as electricity penetrates the skin, and the full extent of electrical burns are often obscured. They will also cause extensive destruction of tissue at the entry and exit points. Electrical injuries in the home are often minor, while high tension power cables cause serious electrical injuries in the workplace. <a href="/wiki/Lightning_strike" title="Lightning strike">Lightning strikes</a> can also cause severe electrical injuries. Fatal electrical injuries are often caused by tetanic spasm inducing <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_arrest" title="Respiratory arrest">respiratory arrest</a> or interference with the heart causing <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_arrest" title="Cardiac arrest">cardiac arrest</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Chemical_burn" title="Chemical burn">Chemical burns</a> are caused by contact with <a href="/wiki/Corrosive_substance" title="Corrosive substance">corrosive substances</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acid</a> or <a href="/wiki/Alkali" title="Alkali">alkali</a>. Chemical burns are rarer than most other burns, though there are many chemicals that can damage tissue. The most common chemical-related injuries are those caused by <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>. Some <a href="/wiki/Chemical_weapon" title="Chemical weapon">chemical weapons</a> induce chemical burns, such as <a href="/wiki/White_phosphorus_munitions" title="White phosphorus munitions">white phosphorus</a>. Most chemical burns are treated with extensive application of water to remove the chemical contaminant, though some burn-inducing chemicals react with water to create more severe injuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The ingestion of corrosive substances can cause chemical burns to the larynx and stomach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_mechanisms">Other mechanisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Injury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Other mechanisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Toxic_injury" title="Toxic injury">Toxic injury</a> is caused by the ingestion, inhalation, injection, or absorption of a <a href="/wiki/Toxin" title="Toxin">toxin</a>. This may occur through an interaction caused by a <a href="/wiki/Drug" title="Drug">drug</a> or the ingestion of a <a href="/wiki/Poison" title="Poison">poison</a>. Different toxins may cause different types of injuries, and many will cause injury to specific organs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Toxins in gases, dusts, aerosols, and smoke can be inhaled, potentially causing respiratory failure. Respiratory toxins can be released by structural fires, industrial accidents, domestic mishaps, or through chemical weapons. Some toxicants may affect other parts of the body after inhalation, such as carbon monoxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Asphyxia causes injury to the body from a lack of oxygen. It can be caused by <a href="/wiki/Drowning" title="Drowning">drowning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inhalation" title="Inhalation">inhalation</a> of certain substances, <a href="/wiki/Strangling" title="Strangling">strangulation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Foreign_body_aspiration" title="Foreign body aspiration">blockage of the airway</a>, traumatic injury to the airway, <a href="/wiki/Apnea" title="Apnea">apnea</a>, and other means. The most immediate injury caused by asphyxia is <a href="/wiki/Hypoxia_(medical)" title="Hypoxia (medical)">hypoxia</a>, which can in turn cause acute lung injury or <a href="/wiki/Acute_respiratory_distress_syndrome" title="Acute respiratory distress syndrome">acute respiratory distress syndrome</a> as well as damage to the circulatory system. The most severe injury associated with asphyxiation is cerebral <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_hypoxia" title="Cerebral hypoxia">hypoxia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brain_ischemia" title="Brain ischemia">ischemia</a>, in which the brain receives insufficient oxygen or blood, resulting in neurological damage or death. Specific injuries are associated with water inhalation, including alveolar collapse, <a href="/wiki/Atelectasis" title="Atelectasis">atelectasis</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intrapulmonary_shunting" title="Intrapulmonary shunting">intrapulmonary shunting</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ventilation_perfusion_mismatch" title="Ventilation perfusion mismatch">ventilation perfusion mismatch</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Simple asphyxia is caused by a lack of external oxygen supply. Systemic asphyxia is caused by exposure to a compound that prevents oxygen from being transported or used by the body. This can be caused by <a href="/wiki/Azide" title="Azide">azides</a>, carbon monoxide, <a href="/wiki/Cyanide" title="Cyanide">cyanide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smoke_inhalation" title="Smoke inhalation">smoke inhalation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methemoglobinemia" title="Methemoglobinemia">methemoglobinemia</a>-inducing substances, <a href="/wiki/Opioid" title="Opioid">opioids</a>, or other systemic asphyxiants. Ventilation and oxygenation are necessary for treatment of asphyxiation, and some asphyxiants can be treated with antidotes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>Injuries of overuse or overexertion can occur when the body is strained through use, affecting the bones, muscles, ligaments, or tendons. <a href="/wiki/Sports_injury" title="Sports injury">Sports injuries</a> are often overuse injuries such as <a href="/wiki/Tendinopathy" title="Tendinopathy">tendinopathy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Over-extension of the ligaments and tendons can result in <a href="/wiki/Sprain" title="Sprain">sprains</a> and <a href="/wiki/Strain_(injury)" title="Strain (injury)">strains</a>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-25">[25]</a></sup> Repetitive sedentary behaviors such as extended use of a computer or a physically repetitive occupation may cause a <a href="/wiki/Repetitive_strain_injury" title="Repetitive strain injury">repetitive strain injury</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Extended use of brightly lit screens may also cause <a href="/wiki/Eye_strain" title="Eye strain">eye strain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Locations">Locations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Injury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Locations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abdomen">Abdomen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Injury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Abdomen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Abdominal_trauma" title="Abdominal trauma">Abdominal trauma</a> includes injuries to the <a href="/wiki/Stomach" title="Stomach">stomach</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intestines" title="Intestines">intestines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pancreas" title="Pancreas">pancreas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">kidneys</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gallbladder" title="Gallbladder">gallbladder</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Spleen" title="Spleen">spleen</a>. Abdominal injuries are typically caused by traffic accidents, assaults, falls, and work-related injuries, and physical examination is often unreliable in diagnosing blunt abdominal trauma. <a href="/wiki/Splenic_injury" title="Splenic injury">Splenic injury</a> can cause <a href="/wiki/Hypovolemia" title="Hypovolemia">low blood volume</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hemoperitoneum" title="Hemoperitoneum">blood in the peritoneal cavity</a>. The treatment and prognosis of splenic injuries are dependent on cardiovascular stability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The gallbladder is rarely injured in blunt trauma, occurring in about 2% of blunt abdominal trauma cases. Injuries to the gallbladder are typically associated with injuries to other abdominal organs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> The intestines are susceptible to injury following blunt abdominal trauma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The kidneys are protected by other structures in the abdomen, and most injuries to the kidney are a result of blunt trauma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Kidney injuries typically cause blood in the urine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to its location in the body, <a href="/wiki/Pancreatic_injury" title="Pancreatic injury">pancreatic injury</a> is relatively uncommon but more difficult to diagnose. Most injuries to the pancreas are caused by penetrative trauma, such as gunshot wounds and stab wounds. Pancreatic injuries occur in under 5% of blunt abdominal trauma cases. The severity of pancreatic injury depends primarily on the amount of harm caused to the <a href="/wiki/Pancreatic_duct" title="Pancreatic duct">pancreatic duct</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The stomach is also well protected from injury due to its heavy layering, its extensive blood supply, and its position relative to the <a href="/wiki/Rib_cage" title="Rib cage">rib cage</a>. As with pancreatic injuries, most traumatic stomach injuries are caused by penetrative trauma, and most civilian weapons do not cause long-term tissue damage to the stomach. Blunt trauma injuries to the stomach are typically caused by traffic accidents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Ingestion of corrosive substances can cause chemical burns to the stomach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-19">[19]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Liver_injury" title="Liver injury">Liver injury</a> is the most common type of organ damage in cases of abdominal trauma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-:8-35">[35]</a></sup> The liver's size and location in the body makes injury relatively common compared to other abdominal organs, and blunt trauma injury to the liver is typically treated with nonoperative management.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Liver injuries are rarely serious, though most injuries to the liver are concomitant with other injuries, particularly to the spleen, ribs, pelvis, or spinal cord.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-:8-35">[35]</a></sup> The liver is also susceptible to toxic injury, with overdose of <a href="/wiki/Paracetamol" title="Paracetamol">paracetamol</a> being a common cause of liver failure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Face">Face</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Injury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Face">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Black_eye_(2).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1880" data-file-width="2096" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Black_eye_%282%29.jpg/200px-Black_eye_%282%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Black_eye_%282%29.jpg/300px-Black_eye_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Black_eye_%282%29.jpg/400px-Black_eye_%282%29.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Black_eye_(2).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Black_eye" title="Black eye">black eye</a>, a common facial injury.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Facial_trauma" title="Facial trauma">Facial trauma</a> may affect the <a href="/wiki/Eye" title="Eye">eyes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nose" title="Nose">nose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ear" title="Ear">ears</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Mouth" title="Mouth">mouth</a>. Nasal trauma is a common injury and the most common type of facial injury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Oral injuries are typically caused by traffic accidents or alcohol-related violence, though falls are a more common cause in young children. The primary concerns regarding oral injuries are that the airway is clear and that there are no concurrent injuries to other parts of the head or neck. Oral injuries may occur in the soft tissue of the face, the hard tissue of the mandible, or as <a href="/wiki/Dental_trauma" title="Dental trauma">dental trauma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>The ear is susceptible to trauma in head injuries due to its prominent location and exposed structure. Ear injuries may be internal or external. Injuries of the external ear are typically lacerations of the cartilage or the formation of a hematoma. Injuries of the middle and internal ear may include a <a href="/wiki/Perforated_eardrum" title="Perforated eardrum">perforated eardrum</a> or trauma caused by extreme pressure changes. The ear is also highly sensitive to <a href="/wiki/Blast_injury" title="Blast injury">blast injury</a>. The bones of the ear are connected to facial nerves, and ear injuries can cause paralysis of the face. Trauma to the ear can cause <a href="/wiki/Hearing_loss" title="Hearing loss">hearing loss</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Eye_injury" title="Eye injury">Eye injuries</a> often take place in the <a href="/wiki/Cornea" title="Cornea">cornea</a>, and they have the potential to permanently damage vision. <a href="/wiki/Corneal_abrasion" title="Corneal abrasion">Corneal abrasions</a> are a common injury caused by contact with foreign objects. The eye can also be injured by a foreign object remaining in the cornea. Radiation damage can be caused by exposure to excessive light, often caused by <a href="/wiki/Welding" title="Welding">welding</a> without eye protection or being exposed to excessive ultraviolet radiation, such as <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a>. Exposure to corrosive chemicals can permanently damage the eyes, causing <a href="/wiki/Visual_impairment" title="Visual impairment">blindness</a> if not sufficiently irrigated. The eye is protected from most blunt injuries by the <a href="/wiki/Infraorbital_margin" title="Infraorbital margin">infraorbital margin</a>, but in some cases blunt force may cause an eye to hemorrhage or tear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Overuse of the eyes can cause eye strain, particularly when looking at brightly lit screens for an extended period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heart">Heart</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Injury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Heart">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Cardiac injuries affect the <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">blood vessels</a>. <a href="/wiki/Blunt_cardiac_injury" title="Blunt cardiac injury">Blunt cardiac injury</a> in a common injury caused by blunt trauma to the heart. It can be difficult to diagnose, and it can have many effects on the heart, including contusions, ruptures, acute valvular disorders, <a href="/wiki/Arrhythmia" title="Arrhythmia">arrhythmia</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Heart_failure" title="Heart failure">heart failure</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Penetrative trauma to the heart is typically caused by stab wounds or gunshot wounds. Accidental cardiac penetration can also occur in rare cases from a fractured sternum or rib. Stab wounds to the heart are typically survivable with medical attention, though gunshot wounds to the heart are not. The <a href="/wiki/Ventricle_(heart)" title="Ventricle (heart)">right ventricle</a> is most susceptible to injury due to its prominent location. The two primary consequences of traumatic injury to the heart are severe hemorrhaging and <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_tamponade" title="Cardiac tamponade">fluid buildup</a> around the heart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Musculoskeletal">Musculoskeletal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Injury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Musculoskeletal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Types_of_fracture.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="491" data-file-width="929" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Types_of_fracture.jpg/300px-Types_of_fracture.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Types_of_fracture.jpg/450px-Types_of_fracture.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Types_of_fracture.jpg/600px-Types_of_fracture.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Types_of_fracture.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Types of bone fracture</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Musculoskeletal_injury" title="Musculoskeletal injury">Musculoskeletal injuries</a> affect the <a href="/wiki/Human_skeleton" title="Human skeleton">skeleton</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Muscular_system" title="Muscular system">muscular system</a>. <a href="/wiki/Soft_tissue_injury" title="Soft tissue injury">Soft tissue injuries</a> affect the <a href="/wiki/Skeletal_muscle" title="Skeletal muscle">skeletal muscles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ligament" title="Ligament">ligaments</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tendon" title="Tendon">tendons</a>. Ligament and tendon injuries account for half of all musculoskeletal injuries. Ligament sprains and tendon strains are common injuries that do not require intervention, but the healing process is slow. <a href="/wiki/Physical_therapy" title="Physical therapy">Physical therapy</a> can be used to assist reconstruction and use of injured ligaments and tendons. Torn ligaments or tendons typically require surgery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-:7-25">[25]</a></sup> Skeletal muscles are abundant in the body and commonly injured when engaging in athletic activity. Muscle injuries trigger an inflammatory response to facilitate healing. Blunt trauma to the muscles can cause contusions and <a href="/wiki/Hematoma" title="Hematoma">hematomas</a>. Excessive tensile strength can overstretch a muscle, causing a strain. Strains may present with torn muscle fibers, hemorrhaging, or <a href="/wiki/Edema" title="Edema">fluid in the muscles</a>. Severe muscle injuries in which a tear extends across the muscle can cause total loss of function. Penetrative trauma can cause laceration to muscles, which may take an extended time to heal. Unlike contusions and strains, lacerations are uncommon in sports injuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Traumatic injury may cause various <a href="/wiki/Bone_fracture" title="Bone fracture">bone fractures</a> depending on the amount of force, direction of the force, and width of the area affected. <a href="/wiki/Pathologic_fracture" title="Pathologic fracture">Pathologic fractures</a> occur when a previous condition weakens the bone until it can be easily fractured. <a href="/wiki/Stress_fracture" title="Stress fracture">Stress fractures</a> occur when the bone is overused or suffers under excessive or traumatic pressure, often during athletic activity. Hematomas occur immediately following a bone fracture, and the healing process often takes from six weeks to three months to complete, though continued use of the fractured bone will prevent healing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Articular_cartilage_damage" title="Articular cartilage damage">Articular cartilage damage</a> may also affect function of the skeletal system, and it can cause posttraumatic <a href="/wiki/Osteoarthritis" title="Osteoarthritis">osteoarthritis</a>. Unlike most bodily structures, cartilage cannot be healed once it is damaged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nervous_system">Nervous system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Injury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Nervous system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Parachemableedwithedema.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="977" data-file-width="831" decoding="async" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Parachemableedwithedema.png/220px-Parachemableedwithedema.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Parachemableedwithedema.png/330px-Parachemableedwithedema.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Parachemableedwithedema.png/440px-Parachemableedwithedema.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Parachemableedwithedema.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/CT_scan" title="CT scan">CT scan</a> of a brain hemorrhage (bottom arrow) and the surrounding <a href="/wiki/Edema" title="Edema">edema</a> (top arrow)</div></div></div>
<p>Injuries to the <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">nervous system</a> include <a href="/wiki/Brain_damage" title="Brain damage">brain injury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spinal_cord_injury" title="Spinal cord injury">spinal cord injury</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nerve_injury" title="Nerve injury">nerve injury</a>. Trauma to the <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a> causes <a href="/wiki/Traumatic_brain_injury" title="Traumatic brain injury">traumatic brain injury</a> (TBI), causing "long-term physical, emotional, behavioral, and cognitive consequences". Mild TBI, including <a href="/wiki/Concussion" title="Concussion">concussion</a>, often occurs during athletic activity, military service, or as a result of untreated <a href="/wiki/Epilepsy" title="Epilepsy">epilepsy</a>, and its effects are typically short-term. More severe injuries to the brain cause moderate TBI, which may cause confusion or lethargy, or severe TBI, which may result in a <a href="/wiki/Coma" title="Coma">coma</a> or a secondary brain injury. TBI is a leading cause of mortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Approximately half of all trauma-related deaths involve TBI.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-12">[12]</a></sup> Non-traumatic injuries to the brain cause<a href="/wiki/Acquired_brain_injury" title="Acquired brain injury">acquired brain injury</a> (ABI). This can be caused by <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">stroke</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Brain_tumor" title="Brain tumor">brain tumor</a>, poison, infection, cerebral hypoxia, drug use, or the secondary effect of a TBI.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Injury to the <a href="/wiki/Spinal_cord" title="Spinal cord">spinal cord</a> is not immediately terminal, but it is associated with concomitant injuries, lifelong medical complications, and reduction in life expectancy. It may result in complications in several major organ systems and a significant reduction in mobility or paralysis. <a href="/wiki/Spinal_shock" title="Spinal shock">Spinal shock</a> causes temporary paralysis and loss of reflexes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Unlike most other injuries, damage to the <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_nervous_system" title="Peripheral nervous system">peripheral nerves</a> is not healed through cellular proliferation. Following nerve injury, the nerves undergo degeneration before regenerating, and other pathways can be strengthened or reprogrammed to make up for lost function. The most common form of peripheral nerve injury is stretching, due to their inherent elasticity. Nerve injuries may also be caused by laceration or compression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pelvis">Pelvis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Injury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Pelvis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Injuries to the <a href="/wiki/Pelvic_cavity" title="Pelvic cavity">pelvic area</a> include injuries to the <a href="/wiki/Urinary_bladder" title="Urinary bladder">bladder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rectum" title="Rectum">rectum</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colon_(anatomy)" title="Colon (anatomy)">colon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Human_reproductive_system" title="Human reproductive system">reproductive organs</a>. Traumatic injury to the bladder is rare and often occurs with other injuries to the abdomen and pelvis. The bladder is protected by the <a href="/wiki/Peritoneum" title="Peritoneum">peritoneum</a>, and most cases of bladder injury are concurrent with a fracture of the <a href="/wiki/Pelvis" title="Pelvis">pelvis</a>. Bladder trauma typically causes <a href="/wiki/Hematuria" title="Hematuria">hematuria</a>, or blood in the urine. Ingestion of alcohol may cause distension of the bladder, increasing the risk of injury. A <a href="/wiki/Catheter" title="Catheter">catheter</a> may be used to extract blood from the bladder in the case of hemorrhaging, though injuries that break the peritoneum typically require surgery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The colon is rarely injured by blunt trauma, with most cases occurring from penetrative trauma through the abdomen. Rectal injury is less common than injury to the colon, though the rectum is more susceptible to injury following blunt force trauma to the pelvis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Injuries to the <a href="/wiki/Male_reproductive_system" title="Male reproductive system">male reproductive system</a> are rarely fatal and typically treatable through grafts and reconstruction. The elastic nature of the <a href="/wiki/Scrotum" title="Scrotum">scrotum</a> makes it resistant to injury, accounting for 1% of traumatic injuries. Trauma to the scrotum may cause damage to the <a href="/wiki/Testicle" title="Testicle">testis</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Spermatic_cord" title="Spermatic cord">spermatic cord</a>. Trauma to the <a href="/wiki/Penis" title="Penis">penis</a> can cause <a href="/wiki/Penile_fracture" title="Penile fracture">penile fracture</a>, typically as a result of vigorous <a href="/wiki/Sexual_intercourse" title="Sexual intercourse">intercourse</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Injuries to the <a href="/wiki/Female_reproductive_system" title="Female reproductive system">female reproductive system</a> are often a result of <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">pregnancy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Childbirth" title="Childbirth">childbirth</a> or sexual activity. They are rarely fatal, but they can produce a variety of complications, such as chronic discomfort, <a href="/wiki/Dyspareunia" title="Dyspareunia">dyspareunia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Infertility" title="Infertility">infertility</a>, or the formation of <a href="/wiki/Fistula" title="Fistula">fistulas</a>. Age can greatly affect the nature of genital injuries in women due to changes in hormone composition. Childbirth is the most common cause of genital injury to women of reproductive age. Many cultures practice <a href="/wiki/Female_genital_mutilation" title="Female genital mutilation">female genital mutilation</a>, which is estimated to affect over 125 million women and girls worldwide as of 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Tears and abrasions to the <a href="/wiki/Vagina" title="Vagina">vagina</a> are common during sexual intercourse, and these may be exacerbated in instances of non-consensual sexual activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Respiratory_tract">Respiratory tract</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Injury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Respiratory tract">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Injuries to the <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_tract" title="Respiratory tract">respiratory tract</a> affect the <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lungs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thoracic_diaphragm" title="Thoracic diaphragm">diaphragm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trachea" title="Trachea">trachea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bronchus" title="Bronchus">bronchus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pharynx" title="Pharynx">pharynx</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Larynx" title="Larynx">larynx</a>. <a href="/wiki/Tracheobronchial_injury" title="Tracheobronchial injury">Tracheobronchial injuries</a> are rare and often associated with other injuries. <a href="/wiki/Bronchoscopy" title="Bronchoscopy">Bronchoscopy</a> is necessary for an accurate diagnosis of tracheobronchial injury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> The neck, including the pharynx and larynx, is highly vulnerable to injury due to its complex, compacted anatomy. Injuries to this area can cause <a href="/wiki/Airway_obstruction" title="Airway obstruction">airway obstruction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Ingestion of corrosive chemicals can cause chemical burns to the larynx.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-19">[19]</a></sup> Inhalation of toxic materials can also cause serious injury to the respiratory tract.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Severe trauma to the chest can cause damage to the lungs, including <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_contusion" title="Pulmonary contusion">pulmonary contusions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hemothorax" title="Hemothorax">accumulation of blood</a>, or a <a href="/wiki/Pneumothorax" title="Pneumothorax">collapsed lung</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammation</a> response to a lung injury can cause acute respiratory distress syndrome. Injuries to the lungs may cause symptoms ranging from <a href="/wiki/Shortness_of_breath" title="Shortness of breath">shortness of breath</a> to terminal <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_failure" title="Respiratory failure">respiratory failure</a>. Injuries to the lungs are often fatal, and survivors often have a reduced quality of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Injuries to the diaphragm are uncommon and rarely serious, but blunt trauma to the diaphragm can result in the formation of a <a href="/wiki/Hernia" title="Hernia">hernia</a> over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Injuries to the diaphragm may present in many ways, including abnormal blood pressure, cardiac arrest, gastroinetestinal obstruction, and respiratory insufficiency. Injuries to the diaphragm are often associated with other injuries in the chest or abdomen, and its position between two major cavities of the human body may complicate diagnosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Skin">Skin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Injury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Skin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most injuries to the skin are minor and do not require specialist treatment. Lacerations of the skin are typically repaired with <a href="/wiki/Surgical_suture" title="Surgical suture">sutures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Surgical_staple" title="Surgical staple">staples</a>, or adhesives. The skin is susceptible to burns, and burns to the skin often cause blistering. Abrasive trauma scrapes or rubs off the skin, and severe abrasions require <a href="/wiki/Skin_grafting" title="Skin grafting">skin grafting</a> to repair. Skin tears involve the removal of the epidermis or dermis through friction or shearing forces, often in vulnerable populations such as the elderly. Skin injuries are potentially complicated by foreign bodies such as glass, metal, or dirt that entered the wound, and skin wounds often require cleaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment">Treatment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Injury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Treatment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_041117-M-0000G-004_U.S._Navy_Surgeons_and_Hospital_Corpsman,_assigned_to_the_Surgical-Shock_Trauma_Platoon_(SSTP)_at_Camp_Taqaddum,_Iraq,_operate_on_a_Marine.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/US_Navy_041117-M-0000G-004_U.S._Navy_Surgeons_and_Hospital_Corpsman%2C_assigned_to_the_Surgical-Shock_Trauma_Platoon_%28SSTP%29_at_Camp_Taqaddum%2C_Iraq%2C_operate_on_a_Marine.jpg/250px-thumbnail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/US_Navy_041117-M-0000G-004_U.S._Navy_Surgeons_and_Hospital_Corpsman%2C_assigned_to_the_Surgical-Shock_Trauma_Platoon_%28SSTP%29_at_Camp_Taqaddum%2C_Iraq%2C_operate_on_a_Marine.jpg/375px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/US_Navy_041117-M-0000G-004_U.S._Navy_Surgeons_and_Hospital_Corpsman%2C_assigned_to_the_Surgical-Shock_Trauma_Platoon_%28SSTP%29_at_Camp_Taqaddum%2C_Iraq%2C_operate_on_a_Marine.jpg/500px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_041117-M-0000G-004_U.S._Navy_Surgeons_and_Hospital_Corpsman,_assigned_to_the_Surgical-Shock_Trauma_Platoon_(SSTP)_at_Camp_Taqaddum,_Iraq,_operate_on_a_Marine.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Trauma surgeons operating on a United States Marine</div></div></div>
<p>Much of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_practice" title="Medical practice">medical practice</a> is dedicated to the treatment of injuries. <a href="/wiki/Traumatology" title="Traumatology">Traumatology</a> is the study of traumatic injuries and injury repair. Certain injuries may be treated by <a href="/wiki/Medical_specialty" title="Medical specialty">specialists</a>. Serious injuries sometimes require <a href="/wiki/Trauma_surgery" title="Trauma surgery">trauma surgery</a>. Following serious injuries, physical therapy and <a href="/wiki/Occupational_therapy" title="Occupational therapy">occupational therapy</a> are sometimes used for rehabilitation. <a href="/wiki/Medication" title="Medication">Medication</a> is commonly used to treat injuries.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Emergency_medicine" title="Emergency medicine">Emergency medicine</a> during major trauma prioritizes the immediate consideration of life-threatening injuries that can be quickly addressed. The airway is evaluated, clearing bodily fluids with suctioning or creating an artificial airway if necessary. Breathing is evaluated by evaluating motion of the chest wall and checking for <a href="/wiki/Hemothorax" title="Hemothorax">blood</a> or <a href="/wiki/Pneumothorax" title="Pneumothorax">air</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Pleural_cavity" title="Pleural cavity">pleural cavity</a>. Circulation is evaluated to resuscitate the patient, including the application of <a href="/wiki/Intravenous_therapy" title="Intravenous therapy">intravenous therapy</a>. Disability is evaluated by checking for responsiveness and reflexes. Exposure is then used to examine the patient for external injury. Following immediate life-saving procedures, a <a href="/wiki/CT_scan" title="CT scan">CT scan</a> is used for a more thorough diagnosis. Further resuscitation may be required, including ongoing blood transfusion, mechanical ventilation and nutritional support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-:3-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pain_management" title="Pain management">Pain management</a> is another aspect of injury treatment. <a href="/wiki/Pain" title="Pain">Pain</a> serves as an indicator to determine the nature and severity of an injury, but it can also worsen an injury, reduce mobility, and affect quality of life. <a href="/wiki/Analgesic" title="Analgesic">Analgesic</a> drugs are used to reduce the pain associated with injuries, depending on the person's age, the severity of the injury, and previous medical conditions that may affect pain relief. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NSAID" title="NSAID">NSAIDs</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Aspirin" title="Aspirin">aspirin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ibuprofen" title="Ibuprofen">ibuprofen</a> are commonly used for acute pain. Opioid medications such as <a href="/wiki/Fentanyl" title="Fentanyl">fentanyl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methadone" title="Methadone">methadone</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Morphine" title="Morphine">morphine</a> are used to treat severe pain in major trauma, but their use is limited due to associated long-term risks such as addiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Complications">Complications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Injury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Complications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Complication_(medicine)" title="Complication (medicine)">Complications</a> may arise as a result of certain injuries, increasing the recovery time, further exasperating the symptoms, or potentially causing death. The extent of the injury and the age of the injured person may contribute to the likelihood of complications. Infection of wounds is a common complication in traumatic injury, resulting in diagnoses such as <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sepsis" title="Sepsis">sepsis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Wound infection prevents the healing process from taking place and can cause further damage to the body. A majority of wounds are contaminated with microbes from other parts of the body, and infection takes place when the immune system is unable to address this contamination. The surgical removing of devitalized tissue and the use of topical antimicrobial agents can prevent infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hemorrhaging of blood is a common result of injuries, and it can cause several complications. Pooling of blood under the skin can cause a <a href="/wiki/Hematoma" title="Hematoma">hematoma</a>, particularly after blunt trauma or the suture of a laceration. Hematomas are susceptible to infection and are typically treated compression, though surgery is necessary in severe cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Excessive blood loss can cause <a href="/wiki/Hypovolemic_shock" title="Hypovolemic shock">hypovolemic shock</a> in which cellular oxygenation can no longer take place. This can cause <a href="/wiki/Tachycardia" title="Tachycardia">tachycardia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypotension" title="Hypotension">hypotension</a>, coma, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organ_failure" title="Organ failure">organ failure</a>. <a href="/wiki/Fluid_replacement" title="Fluid replacement">Fluid replacement</a> is often necessary to treat blood loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Other complications of injuries include cavitation, development of fistulas, and organ failure.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_and_psychological_aspects">Social and psychological aspects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Injury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Social and psychological aspects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Injuries often cause psychological harm in addition to physical harm. Traumatic injuries are associated with psychological trauma and distress, and some victims of traumatic injuries will display symptoms of <a href="/wiki/Post-traumatic_stress_disorder" title="Post-traumatic stress disorder">post-traumatic stress disorder</a> during and after the recovery of the injury. The specific symptoms and their <a href="/wiki/Trauma_trigger" title="Trauma trigger">triggers</a> vary depending on the nature of the injury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Body_image" title="Body image">Body image</a> and <a href="/wiki/Self-esteem" title="Self-esteem">self-esteem</a> can also be affected by injury. Injuries that cause permanent disabilities, such as spinal cord injuries, can have severe effects on self-esteem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Disfiguring injuries can negatively affect body image, leading to a lower quality of life. Burn injuries in particular can cause dramatic changes in a person's appearance that may negatively affect body image.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Severe injury can also cause social harm. Disfiguring injuries may also result in stigma due to scarring or other changes in appearance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Certain injuries may necessitate a change in occupation or prevent employment entirely. Leisure activities are similarly limited, and athletic activities in particular may be impossible following severe injury. In some cases, the effects of injury may strain personal relationships, such as marriages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Psychological and social variables have been found to affect the likelihood of injuries among athletes. Increased life stress can cause an increase in the likelihood of athletic injury, while <a href="/wiki/Social_support" title="Social support">social support</a> can decrease the likelihood of injury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Social support also assists in the recovery process after athletic injuries occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFOrchardMeeuwisseDermanHägglund2020">Orchard, JW; Meeuwisse, W; Derman, W; Hägglund, M; Soligard, T; Schwellnus, M;
